   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    List of natural satellites
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

       </a>

      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       's planets and officially recognized dwarf planets are known to be orbited by 184
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        natural satellites
       </a>
       , or moons. 19 moons in the Solar System are large enough to be gravitationally rounded, and thus would be considered
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">
        planets
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwarf_planets" title="Dwarf planets">
        dwarf planets
       </a>
       if they were in direct orbit around the Sun.
      </p>
      <p>
       Moons are classed in two separate categories according to their orbits:
       <a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon">
        regular
       </a>
       moons, which have
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prograde_orbit" title="Prograde orbit">
        prograde orbits
       </a>
       (they orbit in the direction of their planets' rotation) and lie close to the plane of their equators, and
       <a href="/wiki/Irregular_moon" title="Irregular moon">
        irregular
       </a>
       moons, whose orbits can be pro- or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">
        retrograde
       </a>
       (against the direction of their planets' rotation) and often lie at extreme angles to their planets' equators. Irregular moons are probably minor planets that have been captured from surrounding space. Most irregular moons are less than 10 kilometres (6.2 mi) in diameter.
      </p>
      <p>
       The earliest published discovery of a moon other than the Earth's was by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       , who discovered the four
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       orbiting
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       in 1610. Over the following three centuries only a few more moons were discovered. Missions to other planets in the 1970s, most notably the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
         Voyager 1
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         2
        </a>
       </i>
       missions, saw a surge in the number of moons detected, and observations since the year 2000, using mostly large, ground-based
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">
        optical telescopes
       </a>
       , have discovered many more, all of which are irregular.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Moons_by_primary">
        Moons by primary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_natural_satellites&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Moons by primary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5786" data-file-width="10256" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg/400px-Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg/600px-Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg/800px-Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg 2x" width="400"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Small_bodies_of_the_Solar_System.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Some moons, minor planets and comets of the Solar System to scale
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1700" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg/300px-Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg/450px-Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg/600px-Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Selected moons, with Earth to scale. Nineteen moons are large enough to be round, and one, Titan, has a substantial atmosphere.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moons_vs_time.SVG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="600" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Moons_vs_time.SVG/300px-Moons_vs_time.SVG.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Moons_vs_time.SVG/450px-Moons_vs_time.SVG.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Moons_vs_time.SVG/600px-Moons_vs_time.SVG.png 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moons_vs_time.SVG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The number of moons discovered in each year
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       , the smallest and innermost planet, has no moons, or at least none that can be detected to a diameter of 1.6 km (1.0 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mercury_no_moons_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mercury_no_moons-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For a very short time in 1974, Mercury
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_moon" title="Mercury's moon">
        was thought to have a moon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       also has no moons,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-venus_no_moons_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-venus_no_moons-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though
       <a href="/wiki/Neith_(hypothetical_moon)" title="Neith (hypothetical moon)">
        reports of a moon around Venus
       </a>
       have circulated since the 17th century.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       has one
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       , the largest moon of any rocky planet in the Solar System. Earth also has at least two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital" title="Co-orbital">
        co-orbitals
       </a>
       : the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/3753_Cruithne" title="3753 Cruithne">
        3753 Cruithne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/2002_AA29" title="2002 AA29">
        2002 AA
        <sub>
         29
        </sub>
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, since they do not orbit Earth, they are not considered moons. (See
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Other_moons_of_Earth" title="Other moons of Earth">
        Other moons of Earth
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">
        Quasi-satellite
       </a>
       .)
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       has two known moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">
        Phobos
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">
        Deimos
       </a>
       ("fear" and "dread", after attendants of
       <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">
        Ares
       </a>
       , the Greek god of war, equivalent to the Roman Mars). Searches for more satellites have been unsuccessful, putting the maximum radius of any other satellites at 90 m (100 yd).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mars_limit_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mars_limit-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">
        69 moons
       </a>
       with known orbits, of which 60 have confirmed orbits and have thus received permanent designations; of these, 51 have been named. Its eight regular moons are grouped into the planet-sized
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       and the far smaller
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amalthea_group" title="Amalthea group">
        Amalthea group
       </a>
       . They are named after lovers of Zeus, the Greek equivalent of Jupiter. Its 61 known irregular moons are organized into two categories:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_motion" title="Direct motion">
        prograde
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">
        retrograde
       </a>
       . The prograde satellites consist of the
       <a href="/wiki/Himalia_group" title="Himalia group">
        Himalia group
       </a>
       and two others in groups of one. The retrograde moons are grouped into the
       <a href="/wiki/Carme_group" title="Carme group">
        Carme
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ananke_group" title="Ananke group">
        Ananke
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pasiphae_group" title="Pasiphae group">
        Pasiphae
       </a>
       groups, as well as some isolated moons.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       has
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        62 moons
       </a>
       with known orbits; 53 of them have names, and these are the ones with confirmed orbits. Most of them are quite small. Seven moons are large enough to be in
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
        hydrostatic equilibrium
       </a>
       , including
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , the second largest moon in the Solar System. Including these large moons, 24 of Saturn's moons are regular, and traditionally named after
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">
        Titans
       </a>
       or other figures associated with the mythological
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">
        Saturn
       </a>
       . The remaining 38, all small, are irregular, and classified by their orbital characteristics into
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Inuit_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Inuit group of satellites">
        Inuit
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Norse_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Norse group of satellites">
        Norse
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_Gallic_group_of_satellites" title="Saturn's Gallic group of satellites">
        Gallic
       </a>
       groups, and their names are chosen from the corresponding mythologies. The
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        rings of Saturn
       </a>
       are made up of icy objects ranging in size from one centimetre to hundreds of metres, each of which is on its own orbit about the planet. Thus a precise number of Saturnian moons cannot be given, as there is no objective boundary between the countless small anonymous objects that form Saturn's ring system and the larger objects that have been named as moons. At least 150 "
       <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">
        moonlets
       </a>
       " embedded in the rings have been detected by the disturbance they create in the surrounding ring material, though this is thought to be only a small sample of the total population of such objects.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
        Uranus
       </a>
       has 27 named moons, five of which are massive enough to have achieved hydrostatic equilibrium. There are another 13 inner moons that orbit within
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Uranus" title="Rings of Uranus">
        Uranus's ring system
       </a>
       , and another nine outer irregular moons. Unlike most planetary moons, which are named from antiquity, all the moons of Uranus are named after characters from the works of
       <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">
        Shakespeare
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">
        Alexander Pope
       </a>
       's work
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_the_Lock" title="The Rape of the Lock">
         The Rape of the Lock
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">
        Neptune
       </a>
       has 14 moons (one of which is unnamed); the largest,
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       , accounts for more than 99.5 percent of all the mass orbiting the planet. Triton is large enough to have achieved hydrostatic equilibrium, but, uniquely for a large moon, has a retrograde orbit, suggesting it was captured. Neptune also has six known inner regular satellites, and six outer irregular satellites.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
        dwarf planet
       </a>
       , has five moons. Its largest moon
       <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
        Charon
       </a>
       , named after the ferryman who took souls across the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Styx" title="River Styx">
        River Styx
       </a>
       , is more than half as large as Pluto itself, and large enough to orbit a point outside Pluto's surface. In effect, each orbits the other, forming a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">
        binary system
       </a>
       informally referred to as a
       <a href="/wiki/Double_planet" title="Double planet">
        double-dwarf-planet
       </a>
       . Pluto's four other moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">
        Nix
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">
        Hydra
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">
        Kerberos
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">
        Styx
       </a>
       are far smaller and orbit the Pluto–Charon system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2006_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buie_2006-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Among the other dwarf planets,
       <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
        Ceres
       </a>
       has no known moons. It is 90 percent certain that Ceres has no moons larger than 1 km in size, assuming that they would have the same
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       as Ceres itself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ceres_no_moons_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ceres_no_moons-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
        Haumea
       </a>
       has two moons,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi%27iaka_(moon)" title="Hi'iaka (moon)">
        Hi'iaka
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaka_(moon)" title="Namaka (moon)">
        Namaka
       </a>
       , of radii ~195 and ~100 km, respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dumas2011_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dumas2011-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">
        Makemake
       </a>
       has one moon, discovered in April 2016. A satellite having 1% Makemake's brightness would have been detected if it had been located at an
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_distance" title="Angular distance">
        angular distance
       </a>
       from Makemake farther than 0.4 arcseconds (0.0001 degrees).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown2006_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-brown2006-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
        Eris
       </a>
       has one known moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">
        Dysnomia
       </a>
       . Accurately determining its size is difficult: one indicative estimate of its radius is
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7005257000000000000♠
        </span>
        257
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        110 km
       </span>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-johnston-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but on some assumptions could be as high as
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7005342000000000000♠
        </span>
        342
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        25 km
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNOCool4_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TNOCool4-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       object
       <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">
        90482 Orcus
       </a>
       , believed to be a dwarf planet, was found in 2005
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-C8812_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAU-C8812-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to have a natural satellite, later named
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanth_(moon)" title="Vanth (moon)">
        Vanth
       </a>
       . As of October 2009, 190
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_asteroid_moons" title="List of asteroid moons">
        asteroid moons
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mpc-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 63
       <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon#Trans-Neptunian_objects" title="Minor-planet moon">
        trans-Neptunian moons
       </a>
       had been discovered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-mpc-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List">
        List
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_natural_satellites&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: List">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       This is a list of the recognized moons of the planets and of the largest dwarf planets of the Solar System. The 19 moons that are large enough to have been rounded by their own gravity are listed in
       <b>
        bold
       </b>
       . The seven largest moons, which are larger than any of the known
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
        dwarf planets
       </a>
       , are listed in
       <i>
        <b>
         bold and italic
        </b>
       </i>
       . Sidereal period differs from semi-major axis because it depends on the mass of the primary as well as the satellite's distance from it.
      </p>

        <td style="background:black;">
